Social News

The Sea Adventures of American Sea Scouts 212

— Lin

According to Mr. Quimby, the National Director of Sea Scouts in the United States, young people are now inclined towards maritime adventure and romance. This summer, there were five hundred Sea Scout-operated ships traveling between the coasts of the United States and various inland waterways. Last year, the number of Sea Scouts and their leaders throughout the United States increased to 9,800.

Sea Scout training in the Boy Scout movement provides older children with an opportunity for civic and moral training, and gives each Sea Scout the joy of maritime romance and adventure during navigation. Currently, there are 600 Sea Scout groups in 43 states in the United States, as well as Alaska, the Philippines, Hawaii, and Argentina. The Boy Scouts in Mexico and Cuba, which are not connected with the Boy Scouts in the United States, also have Sea Scout organizations. Their results are all quite good.

In the past few years, many children have obtained opportunities for adventure through the Sea Scout movement that would otherwise be difficult to obtain. Last year, the Chicago Sea Scout Corps, operating a small sailboat called the "Blue Moon," won the Mackinac Boat Race Championship, the longest and most difficult freshwater sailing race in the world. Sea Scout Paul Shephard, because he had sufficient Boy Scout training, obtained an opportunity to explore the Arctic with Admiral Byrd. David Martin, a Sea Scout from Austin, explored the Spanish seas with Duke von Luckner.

Since 1927, when the Chicago Sea Scout Corps operated the expedition ship "North Light" of the John Bowden Field Museum on a large-scale expedition to the Arctic, bringing back many museum specimens and scientific studies to Chicago, Sea Scouts have been committed to adventure. However, the adventure of these few Sea Scouts is not enough to represent the entire spirit of Sea Scouts. The value of Sea Scout training, in fact, is to give each Sea Scout an opportunity for adventure under perfect guidance, and to learn seamanship and the maritime changes of the country's history from perfect guidance based on their personal sailing interests.

Ninety percent of the 600 Sea Scout groups in the United States have their own ships. These ships range from small 15-foot boats to 150-foot sailing ships. Boy Scout associations and various Sea Scout groups throughout the United States are equipped with such ships.

Among all the ships in the Sea Scout division of the Boy Scouts of America, many are of historical interest. The old "Argus" clipper, 79 feet long and weighing 38 tons, which the Washington Sea Scouts recently obtained, was formerly a training ship of the United States Naval Academy. The Sea Scouts of San Diego, California, own the old three-masted ship "Star of India," which won honors in the Alaska supply fleet. Speaking of the duration of time, the training ship "Naomi" of the Chicago Boy Scout Headquarters should be considered the oldest of the Sea Scout ships. All of the first Sea Scout leaders in the United States were trained on this ship. The New York City Sea Scouts have a 169-foot sailing ship called the "Princess Minas," which is now used as a racing yacht.

The Sea Scouts in two district areas are equipped with training sailing ships, one in New York and New Jersey, and the other in Illinois, Indiana, Michigan, and Wisconsin. This summer, the Sea Scouts of New York and New Jersey sailed the 55-foot "Tya Stonehead" sailing ship around the sea. The Sea Scouts of the seventh district sailed a 103-foot sailing ship called "Bineta." These two ships sailed at sea for seven days, under the guidance of experienced leaders, allowing the children to learn the basics of sailing and how to operate a ship.

Many Sea Scouts have created their own reputation and anecdotes. Whether operating small boats or large ships, they often have a sincere spirit of service to the local country. Since the addition of the Sea Scout branch, the most notable service spirit among them is the Sea Scouts on the "Salasetai (Southern Cross)" ship. They did a lot of valuable work during the disaster in Florida in the past.

The Trenton Sea Scouts were recently honored to receive a motor speedboat donated by the Robertling family. This ship, called the "Brentley," is 65 feet long and has a Datsel-style engine, making it very outstanding in the Sea Scout fleet. The Sea Scouts of the Klemen township in Michigan have a ship converted from a submarine destroyer, 110 feet long. The Milwaukee Sea Scouts of Wisconsin have an 80-foot sailing ship called the "Captain Lawrence."

The development of Sea Scouts in the Midwest of the United States is very rapid. It seems that in Missouri, Kansas, Iowa, Colorado, Nebraska, and Wyoming, where aquatic activities are rarely mentioned, there are now also 25 Sea Scout troops with about 350 people. Their activities are mostly on the rivers and lakes of their hometowns. In some places where the waterways are too small, they use canoes instead of boats. In order to obtain the necessary knowledge of navigation, the Sea Scouts in this region sometimes sail in small boats to Chicago and the Great Lakes region.

This year, two Sea Scout troops have participated in the "Robert Prince" competition, a total of 570 miles. Various sizes and types of ships can join. The voyage starts from Seattle, Washington, and goes straight to Robert Prince Island in Canada. Two Sea Scout troops participated in this competition, one from the Teai Boy Scouts of Seattle, Washington, and the other from the Sea Scouts of Takoma, Washington. During this competition, there were many old [hands] along the Pacific coast.

Page 24

Navigators participated in it. As a result, the Takoma Sea Scouts won one of the victories. Their caution in sailing won the first prize. At the beginning of this season, the junior and senior members of the Chicago Sea Scout Corps competed in small boat sailing with the junior and senior students of the Great Lakes Naval Training Station. This competition was held in rough seas and the voyage was about one mile long. As a result, the championship of the senior Sea Scouts was taken by the senior sailors, but the junior Sea Scouts defeated the junior sailors.

The American Sea Scouts sailed to many places this year. The San Francisco Sea Scouts sailed on the Sacramento River. The Sea Scouts of the province sailed to New York. Some children came to this big city for the first time. The Trenton Sea Scouts took their new boat to Bangor, giving the members a lot of opportunities for adventure. The Washington Sea Scouts took the Chesapeake Bay to Norfolk, and some Boy Scouts traveled to various places by sea during the summer.

This year, the Chicago Sea Scouts still participated in the Mackinac Island race. This is the longest and most difficult freshwater race in the United States. Last year's championship was taken by a Sea Scout corps, although their ship was very small and had very little chance of winning. According to Mr. Quimby, the National Director of Sea Scouts, the story of the Sea Scouts winning the Mackinac Island race last year is very interesting in the history of the Boy Scout movement.

In 1920, the United States [first] had a Sea Scout organization. At that time, Mr. Quimby served as the Sea Scout instructor on the "Naomi," an old ship obtained by the newly established Chicago Sea Scouts. Mr. Quimby said, "At that time, there were 18 children on this old ship. They didn't know anything about sailing. When the Naomi set sail, some of my friends were driving the exquisite yachts of the yacht club. They laughed at us for teaching children to sail and implement Sea Scout training."

[Driving a ship is a funny thing and also helpless. That was seven years ago, and there were very few Sea Scouts in the country. Last year, when the Mackinac competition was held, the number of Sea Scouts and leaders in the United States reached 7,500, with about 400 ships. Every ship has experienced crew members who know how to drive a ship. The Chicago Sea Scouts have made very rapid progress. Many of the newly established groups have been helped by yacht clubs. These people were the ones who laughed at the Sea Scouts before. The "Blue Moon" ship had only five Sea Scouts at that time.]

[The year before the Mackinac competition was held, these Boy Scouts began to prepare everything. They had to plan carefully because there were many experienced sailors from the Midwest participating in this competition. The Sea Scouts carefully studied the past competition results. They calculated the course of each wind direction to avoid temporary mistakes on the day of the competition. These Boy Scouts unknowingly discovered that the wind in the previous competitions was mostly from the southeast, and this wind always started blowing at the end of the competition.]

[The day of the competition arrived, and a group of neatly dressed Sea Scouts set off on the "Blue Moon" ship with the crowd. A total of forty ships joined this competition. The Sea Scouts joined with their leaders for the first time. On the last day of the competition, everyone was heading to Schuyler's Island. This island is usually used as a turning point in the voyage, and the winner of the competition often takes the lead in this place. The Sea Scouts were lagging behind here, but they had calculated in advance that the wind would suddenly blow from the southeast. The sailors on other ships stopped and re-established their course. The veteran navigators took out their spinnakers, jibs, and fisherman's mizzen sails and happily moved forward quickly.]

[Suddenly, everyone was surprised. A ship flew forward, which had never happened in the competition. This was the ship that the Sea Scouts were riding. The children had already set a clever sailing plan. Their ship also carried a spinnaker, jib, and fisherman's mizzen sail hanging between the fore and aft masts. Because of their self-defined navigation method, the "Blue Moon" ship, representing the Sea Scout division of the Boy Scouts of America, won the championship.] Mr. Quimby said, "If you want to know more, then the members of the Chicago Yacht Club may tell you about the course of the "Blue Moon" winning the championship and the Sea Scout corps they now preside over."
